<div id="review_1995116580" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1995116580" />
    <a title="Peter" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/32760730-peter"><img alt="Peter" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1481428707p2/32760730.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1995116580?book_show_action=true">Jun 16, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Peter" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Peter" href="/user/show/32760730-peter">Peter</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/19346762-too-loud-a-solitude">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32760730-peter?shelf=literary">literary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32760730-peter?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32760730-peter?shelf=five-stars">five-stars</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1995116580" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9237912147955152067">Bohumil Hrabal is a master with words. To understand his mastery, one must know that in addition to novelist, Hrabal is also a Palaverer. I couldn't find a precise definition of the word, other than one who palavers, which is not helpful so I'll give it a go myself. A Palaverer is a literary, grammatical, and story acrobat. More specifically, a Palaverer writes extremely long sentences, sometimes as long as an entire book. They aren't just long for length's sake but also tell a story. Bohumil Hr</span>
  <span id="freeText9237912147955152067" style="display:none">Bohumil Hrabal is a master with words. To understand his mastery, one must know that in addition to novelist, Hrabal is also a Palaverer. I couldn't find a precise definition of the word, other than one who palavers, which is not helpful so I'll give it a go myself. A Palaverer is a literary, grammatical, and story acrobat. More specifically, a Palaverer writes extremely long sentences, sometimes as long as an entire book. They aren't just long for length's sake but also tell a story. Bohumil Hrabal's 'Dancing Lessons for the Advanced in Age' is one example of the craft. When one can perform such great acts on the high wire, walking on the ground is easy. That ease oozes through each sentence in Bohumil Hrabal's non-Palaverer novel, 'Too Loud a Solitude.' <br /><br />A small book with big ideas, 'Too Loud a Solitude' was written during a time when big ideas got one sent to the Gulag. Hrabal is Czech and wrote while the country labored behind the Iron Curtain. The Soviets banned his works until he publicly appeased the government in Moscow --to his supporters' ire.<br /><br />Too Loud a Solitude is an excellent introduction to the author. Hrabal's prose is evidence of his great acrobatic skill, though one is not reading a high-wire act, but rather a very grounded story, beautifully written, about a man toiling away in a basement. His conflict: he's tasked to destroy books and art but adores them above all else. Hrabal saddles this poor man with an additional problem: pride in his work. What's a man to do who prides himself on a job well done but whose job is to destroy the things he loves the most? Read for the cure.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9237912147955152067" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_316916937" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/316916937" />
    <a title="ἀρχαῖος (arkhaîos)(RK)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8930751-arkha-os-rk"><img alt="ἀρχαῖος (arkhaîos)(RK)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1592525719p2/8930751.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/316916937?book_show_action=true">Apr 22, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="ἀρχαῖος (arkhaîos)(RK)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="ἀρχαῖος (arkhaîos)(RK)" href="/user/show/8930751-arkha-os-rk">ἀρχαῖος (arkhaîos)(RK)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8930751-arkha-os-rk?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer316916937" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2648925740956760384">"Once more unto the breach, dear friends, once more...." (Apologies to W.S.)<br/>-<br/>“But just as a beautiful fish will occasionally sparkle in the water of a polluted river that runs through a stretch of factories, so in the flow of old paper the spine of a rare book will occasionally shine forth, and if for a moment I turn away, dazzled, I always turn back in time to rescue it, and after wiping it off on my apron, opening it wide, and breathing in its print, I glue my eyes to the text and read the fir</span>
  <span id="freeText2648925740956760384" style="display:none">"Once more unto the breach, dear friends, once more...." (Apologies to W.S.)<br />-<br />“But just as a beautiful fish will occasionally sparkle in the water of a polluted river that runs through a stretch of factories, so in the flow of old paper the spine of a rare book will occasionally shine forth, and if for a moment I turn away, dazzled, I always turn back in time to rescue it, and after wiping it off on my apron, opening it wide, and breathing in its print, I glue my eyes to the text and read the first sentence like a Homeric prophecy…”<br /><br />-<br />Having spent his life crushing and destroying waste paper and books in his compacting press, Hanta, the protagonist of ‘Too Loud a Solitude’, describes how he finds treasure amongst the waste. Who among us does not know that feeling of excitement at finding an unexpected masterpiece on the shelves of a used book store or a future classic on the site of an online book store? And Hanta has found much to tantalize him: Camus, Sartre, Nietzsche, Homer, Lao-Tze, Holderlin, and “Goethe and Schiller in Morocco bindings.” Over his thirty-five years of compacting old paper and books, he has read them all and, as result, this formally uneducated labourer has become something of an educated fool. His wisdom is matched by his simplicity.<br /><br />-<br />“Along with thirty-five years of pushing the red and the green buttons on my hydraulic press, I’ve had thirty-five years of drinking beer—not that I enjoy it, no, I loathe drunkards, I drink to make me think better, to go to the heart of what I read, because what I read I read not for the fun of it or to kill time or fall asleep: I … drink so that what I read will prevent me from falling into everlasting sleep, will give me the d.t.’s, because I share with Hegel the view that a noble-hearted man is not yet a nobleman, nor a criminal a murderer.” A very wise, but simple, take on Hegel. “If I knew how to write, I’d write a book about the greatest of man’s joys and sorrows.” As would most of us.<br />We follow Hanta as he slowly, much to the ire of his boss, crushes paper into bales to be carted off on trucks; as he creates works of art by framing bales with “sopping-wet Rembrandts, Halses, Monets, Manets, Klimts, Cézannes and other big guns of [reproductions] of European art…” The novel drips with irony as the author, Bohumil Hrabal, shows us the best and the worst of modern society…socialist society of late-twentieth century Czechoslovakia. Every chapter, every paragraph, every sentence of the book must be studied to catch the double entendre, to delineate the kitsch from quality, love from…indifference, reality from illusion, “artist” from “audience.”<br /><br />-<br />‘Too Loud a Solitude’ is a story of love of books by a man who destroys books. It is the story of a man’s love for a woman that seems fated to dissolve in shit (as readers will see). It is violence and what is art. a story of a socialist world where modern “socialist labour” cheerfully marches on to replace all that is out-dated and all that is of value. It is a story of our world where we are unsure of what is violence and what is art. <br /><br />-<br />“Perhaps he was the one who, last year at the Holesovice slaughterhouse, put a knife to my neck, shoved me into a corner, took out a slip of paper, and read me a poem celebrating the beauties of the countryside at Ricany, then apologized, saying he hadn’t found any other way of getting people to listen to his verse."<br /><br />-<br />HIGHLY RECOMMENDED BUT TO BE READ ONLY BY THOSE WITH A STRONG APPRECIATION FOR THE IRONIC.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="2648925740956760384" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_780790144" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/780790144" />
    <a title="Trish" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1826682-trish"><img alt="Trish" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1252295186p2/1826682.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/780790144?book_show_action=true">Dec 04, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Trish" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Trish" href="/user/show/1826682-trish">Trish</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780151904914" href="/book/show/1586146.Too_Loud_a_Solitude">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1826682-trish?shelf=one-for-the-ages">one-for-the-ages</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1826682-trish?shelf=europe">europe</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1826682-trish?shelf=funny">funny</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1826682-trish?shelf=totally-unexpected">totally-unexpected</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1826682-trish?shelf=classics">classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer780790144" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15639532256608612351">Hrabal was quite a man. He seems to have understood, and relished, life's absurdities and joys while criticizing societal failures with the nib of his pen. This work feels fresh enough to have been written today, and paradoxically has the feel of a book a century or two old. His mention, more than once, of the phrase "not until we're totally crushed do we show what we are made of" awakens the reader to the anguish in his "world of moral contradictions."<br/><br/>Nearly every chapter starts with a variatio</span>
  <span id="freeText15639532256608612351" style="display:none">Hrabal was quite a man. He seems to have understood, and relished, life's absurdities and joys while criticizing societal failures with the nib of his pen. This work feels fresh enough to have been written today, and paradoxically has the feel of a book a century or two old. His mention, more than once, of the phrase "not until we're totally crushed do we show what we are made of" awakens the reader to the anguish in his "world of moral contradictions."<br /><br />Nearly every chapter starts with a variation of the phrase, "I've been compacting paper for thirty-five years." This, then is the story of a book baler, who works slowly enough to see the titles of books before they are crushed, and who manages to salvage more than a few. In fact, his salvaged titles are themselves crushed beneath one another in the too small space he has for them above his bed, threatening every day to crush him. <br /><br />But it is only when the boss tells him that he will no longer be pulping old books but blank paper that our book baler feels abandoned. He feels "too loud a solitude" and is so crushed in spirit that he imagines Seneca, author of <i>On Tranquillity of Mind</i>, slashing the veins of his wrist in a gilt bathtub.<br /><br />A coruscating look at the place of art and literature in a socialist state, tempered by the acknowledgement that man himself is perfectible.<br /><br />A <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/278687714?book_show_action=false&amp;page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">far more engaging review</a> of this book is written by Kris. I recommend reading what she wrote.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15639532256608612351" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68030-jenny-reading-envy?shelf=read2017">read2017</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2144618552" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13473787769074648891">I first heard about this book when I listened to episode 185 of the <a href='https://bookfightpod.com/2017/07/10/episode-185-bohumil-hrabal-closely-watched-trains/' rel='nofollow noopener'>Book Fight Podcast</a>, where they discussed the book <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/87282.Closely_Watched_Trains' title='Closely Watched Trains by Bohumil Hrabal' rel='nofollow noopener'>Closely Watched Trains</a>. While describing the background and context of that novel, they mentioned this one in passing, and it sounded right up my alley.<br/><br/>The central character of Hanta works as a trash compactor in the years immediately following the defeat of the Nazis in World War II. He also rescues books from his workplace and has been hoarding and stacking endless books on his</span>
  <span id="freeText13473787769074648891" style="display:none">I first heard about this book when I listened to episode 185 of the <a href="https://bookfightpod.com/2017/07/10/episode-185-bohumil-hrabal-closely-watched-trains/" rel="nofollow noopener">Book Fight Podcast</a>, where they discussed the book <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/87282.Closely_Watched_Trains" title="Closely Watched Trains by Bohumil Hrabal" rel="nofollow noopener">Closely Watched Trains</a>. While describing the background and context of that novel, they mentioned this one in passing, and it sounded right up my alley.<br /><br />The central character of Hanta works as a trash compactor in the years immediately following the defeat of the Nazis in World War II. He also rescues books from his workplace and has been hoarding and stacking endless books on history and philosophy in his tiny apartment. There isn't much more to it, but the framing of this daily life allows for reflections on his part about philosophy, his life, his relationships, his future, etc. I enjoyed the read but probably wouldn't have enjoyed it if had been much longer. And I probably don't know quite enough about philosophy; someone who did would like it even more than me.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13473787769074648891" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/556799001" />
    <a title="Diane S ☔" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4159922-diane-s"><img alt="Diane S ☔" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1598402138p2/4159922.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/556799001?book_show_action=true">Mar 09, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Diane S ☔" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Diane S ☔" href="/user/show/4159922-diane-s">Diane S ☔</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer556799001" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9607342511487530554">This is an irresistible journey through old town Prague, with Hanta, a man whose job is the crushing, compacting and recycling of paper, novels, letters, etc. He steals moments on his job, reading books and acquiring an odd sort of education, becoming quite familiar with the leading philosophers. He takes many of the bales home, using his artistic expression and inserting whole volumes in the middle of the bales. His home, filled with these bales of papers is in danger of crumbling down on him. </span>
  <span id="freeText9607342511487530554" style="display:none">This is an irresistible journey through old town Prague, with Hanta, a man whose job is the crushing, compacting and recycling of paper, novels, letters, etc. He steals moments on his job, reading books and acquiring an odd sort of education, becoming quite familiar with the leading philosophers. He takes many of the bales home, using his artistic expression and inserting whole volumes in the middle of the bales. His home, filled with these bales of papers is in danger of crumbling down on him. He also drinks great quantities of beer. Beer and his books are his life.<br /><br />Prague and it Communist regime had very strident laws of censorship. Whole libraries were crushed, and I can only think how dangerous it must have been for the author to write this novel, which is a thinly veiled criticism of censorship, representing a person's individuality being crushed just as the books were being destroyed.. <br /><br />This novel contained many beautiful passages on reading. ""Because when I read, I don't really read; I pop a beautiful sentence into my mouth and suck it like a fruit drop, or I sip it like a liquor until the thought dissolves in me like alcohol, infusing brain and heart and coursing on through the veins to the root of each blood vessel." Just love this quote. Also this one on the condition of his house, The way I look at it, my life fits together beautifully; at work I have books-and bottles and inkwells and staplers-raining down on me thorough the opening in the cellar ceiling, and at home I have books above me constantly threatening to fall and kill or at least main me." <br /><br />Also found this link to a small view of the short movie of this <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://vimeo.com/11693975">http://vimeo.com/11693975</a><br /><br /> </span>
  <a data-text-id="9607342511487530554" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer4119933209360653114">Is there anything more beautiful than a book about love for books?</span>

<div id="review_604372245" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/604372245" />
    <a title="Anushree Rastogi" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3221198-anushree-rastogi"><img alt="Anushree Rastogi" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1367787426p2/3221198.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/604372245?book_show_action=true">May 01, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Anushree Rastogi" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Anushree Rastogi" href="/user/show/3221198-anushree-rastogi">Anushree Rastogi</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3221198-anushree-rastogi?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer604372245" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5640966320682244974"><b>Before I even begin with the review, I'd like to thank Mohit Parikh, who is forever providing me with books that seem to be available nowhere else.</b><br/><br/><i>“Because when I read, I don’t really read; I pop a beautiful sentence into my mouth and suck it like a fruit drop, or sip it like a liqueur until the thought dissolves in me like alcohol, infusing brain and heart and coursing through the veins to the root of each blood vessel.”</i><br/><br/>I've spent a lifetime cherishing words. Those frugal instruments we have at</span>
  <span id="freeText5640966320682244974" style="display:none"><b>Before I even begin with the review, I'd like to thank Mohit Parikh, who is forever providing me with books that seem to be available nowhere else.</b><br /><br /><i>“Because when I read, I don’t really read; I pop a beautiful sentence into my mouth and suck it like a fruit drop, or sip it like a liqueur until the thought dissolves in me like alcohol, infusing brain and heart and coursing through the veins to the root of each blood vessel.”</i><br /><br />I've spent a lifetime cherishing words. Those frugal instruments we have at hand to express the thoughts that stir our innermost being. The things that caress and comfort and agitate and exasperate. What is reading, after all, but an exercise in appreciating language: that powerful tool that, like everything else we love, fails us only too often, but which we cannot do without. <br /><br />A good book is more than the sum of its parts: it's choice of words, plot, characters. <i>Too Loud A Solitude</i> is much more than just another book. Reading it has been an almost spiritual experience for me. <br /><br />I remembered something I hadn't experienced in years: the joy of finding a truly special book. That moment when I look up at no one in particular and let out a silent yelp of joy: You see that?! This book <i>knows</i> me! The simple pleasures of solitude. <br /><br /><i>“Lost in my dreams, I somehow cross at the traffic signals, bumping into street lamps or people, yet moving onward, exuding fumes of beer and grime, yet smiling, because my briefcase is full of books and that very night I expect them to tell me things about myself I don’t know.”</i> <br /><br /><i>“I can be by myself because I’m never lonely, I’m simply alone, living in my heavily populated solitude, a harum-scarum of infinity and eternity, and Infinity and Eternity seem to take a liking to the likes of me.”</i><br /><br />The books that I have relished the most are the ones that have slowly and silently shaped me into what I am. It is perhaps because of this that I could relate to Hanta, the narrator and protagonist of <i>Too Loud a Solitude</i>. <br /><br /><i>“I am a jug filled with water both magic and plain; I have only to lean over and a stream of beautiful thoughts flows out of me. My education has been so unwitting I can't quite tell which of my thoughts come from me and which from my books, but that's how I've stayed attuned to myself and the world around me.”</i><br /><br />The plot of the book is deceptively simple. <i>Too Loud a Solitude</i> tells the tale of Hanta, a man whose job is to destroy books:the one thing he cherishes in life; compressing into bales all the literature that is deemed by the State as unsuitable. He's been doing this job for thirty five years now, in the same crowded, germ-infested basement. <br /><br />Hanta is not a worldly-wise man. He shows as much interest in pragmatic pursuits as a honey bee in a vinegar cruet. But he knows that destroying books does nothing to stop the flow of ideas. Hrabal very subtly questions the validity of this political censorship. <br /><br /><i>“How much more beautiful it must have been in the days when the only place a thought could make its mark was the human brain and anybody wanting to squelch ideas had to compact human heads, but even that wouldn't have helped, because real thoughts come from outside and travel with us like the noodle soup we take to work; in other words, inquisitors burn books in vain. If a book has anything to say, it burns with a quiet laugh, because any book worth its salt points up and out of itself.”</i><br /><br />Hanta is a man who lives and faces his regrets every day. What an irony to have your daily bread come from destroying that which you love the most!<br /><br /><i>“Rare books perish in my press, under my hands, yet I am unable to stop their flow: I am nothing but a refined butcher.”</i><br /><br />He guzzles down copious quantities of beer in order to keep up with the job he both loves and loathes. He also drinks in order to “go to the heart” of what he reads.<br /><br /><i>“I drink to make me think better, to go to the heart of what I read, because what I read I read not for the fun of it or to kill time or fall asleep; I, who live in a land that has known how to read and write for fifteen generations, drink so that what I read will prevent me from falling into everlasting sleep,”</i><br /><br />yet another reference to the proverbial death of a nation under political duress.<br /><br /><br />All the while, Hanta spirits away the items lined up for destruction,trying to salvage a small part of the fortune he has to destroy each day; although his home is overflowing with books. He reads incessantly, often stealing moments at work to do so. Privately, he sanctifies every bale he makes either by covering the outside with an artistic reproduction or by concealing one of his favorite books in the middle of the bale. <br /><br />The story is full of literary references, spliced in with Hanta's memories and astute observations. The whole thing is told in a spiraling narrative that returns to the heart of the story over and over again, tiding over the many inconsequential yet insightful diversions. <br /><br /><i>“And so everything I see in this world, it all moves backward and forward at the same time, like a blacksmith’s bellows, like everything in my press, turning into its opposite at the command of red and green buttons, and that’s what makes the world go round."</i><br /><br />This, however, does not mean that the tale doesn't have a direction, and an ending, which it does: as appropriate and devastating as one could wish for.<br /><br />Hanta’s solitary bookish life, his dreams of the future, his well-oiled routine – all this comes to an end when he pays a visit to a new compacting plant and sees an enormous apparatus staffed by young workers. In dismay, he watches the entire print-runs of books pass straight from press to pulper, with no one even taking notice. <br /><br />“Like ripping out the innards of live chickens”, Hanta thinks. The larger and more efficient machine also looms as the harbinger of death for smaller presses; and Hanta is quick to realize that he, too, will soon be out of the job that is at the core of his existence. His chosen way out of the impending doom is ultimately tragic: <br /><br /><br /><br /><i>“I refuse to be driven from Paradise, I am in my cellar and no one can turn me out, no one can dismiss me.”</i><br /><br />If his life and labor have been about preserving books and his own soul, Hanta’s suicide becomes a political statement against the mindless pursuit of machine-like efficiency, the destruction of culture and individuality, and totalitarianism overall. <br /><br />My only regret was that I know I would have got so much more out of the book if I knew the slightest thing about Czech culture, literature and history. Well, there’s one way to help that: read more Bohumil Hrabal. <br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5640966320682244974" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_339526123" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/339526123" />
    <a title="Declan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5848260-declan"><img alt="Declan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1427664982p2/5848260.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/339526123?book_show_action=true">May 30, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Declan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Declan" href="/user/show/5848260-declan">Declan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5848260-declan?shelf=eastern-europe">eastern-europe</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer339526123" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1221833931435128154">Hantá loves his job. It gives his life a sense of purpose and within the bounds of what he is commanded to do, he has enough autonomy to feel good about the decisions he makes. His job, which he has undertaken for thirty-five years, is to compress books - which have been confiscated by the state - into neat bales which can be taken away and destroyed. That beauty is a feature of his work comes about because of the love he, a genuine autodidact, has developed for great books and art. He structure</span>
  <span id="freeText1221833931435128154" style="display:none">Hantá loves his job. It gives his life a sense of purpose and within the bounds of what he is commanded to do, he has enough autonomy to feel good about the decisions he makes. His job, which he has undertaken for thirty-five years, is to compress books - which have been confiscated by the state - into neat bales which can be taken away and destroyed. That beauty is a feature of his work comes about because of the love he, a genuine autodidact, has developed for great books and art. He structures each bale with great care: "Last month they delivered nearly fifteen hundred pounds of "Old Masters" reproductions, dropped nearly fifteen hundred pounds of sopping-wet Rembrants, Halses, Monets, Manets, Klimts, Cezannes and other big guns of European art into my cellar, so now I frame each of my bales with reproductions, and when evening comes and the bales stand next to each other in all their splendor for the service elevator, I can't take my eyes off them... I'm the only one on earth who knows that deep in the heart of each bale there's a wide-open Faust or Don Carlos...cushioned on piles of cement bags rests a Thus Spake Zarathustra; I'm the only one on earth who knows which bale has Goethe, which Shiller, which Holderlin, which Nietzsche".<br /><br /> Later he has a consignment of Gaugin reproductions whch he uses to decorate the outside of fifteen bales and when they are loaded on a lorry he says, "I was glad the <i> Bonjour M. Gaugin </i> sides showed above the slats, and I hoped that everyone the truck passed enjoyed it". Hatná also smuggles some of the books home and so, this ordinary, hard working , hard drinking man has a bedroom filled with so many books, piled so high, that he fears that the vibration from one sneeze might bring about his death. <br /><br />The novel is written in a conversational, but intense style and stories unfold through their loose associations: he has a mother, she dies; a man is very cruel to hedgehogs, later curled up like a hedgehog, he dies; a girl he loves has an embarrassing episode at a dance and another at a ski resort. On it goes, like any life, full of puzzles, anxieties, moments of pleasure. His presence in the world matters to a few, most people don't know he's there.Hatná loves books. Every one here loves books. Everyone here will love Hatná. Well maybe that's a fallacy, but it would be difficult not to have great admiration for him and his longing for truth and meaning. <br /><br />The idea of books being compacted into bales and destroyed would once have seemed like an obvious comment on the totalitarian state. Now it has a resonance that extends beyond the political and into the cultural. Book are now threatened by the onward march of the market; bookshops close forever, every week; technology dictates that the book has had it's day. They're old and old is bad. Perhaps that is the way things are going, but for me Hatná is a hero for all times and Too Loud A Solitude is a wonderful example of that beautiful object, a book.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1221833931435128154" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked

    }
  }

  }

Koos vendade Žemtšužnikovidega lõi ta Kozma Prutkovi kuju, kelle lausest „Keegi ei saa hõlmata hõlmamatut“ on saanud käibefraas.\n\n„Vereimejas“ on Tolstoi esimene venekeelne proosatekst ja selles on kõike ülalnimetatut: tragöödiat, balle ja kahtlemata ka hõlmamatut, nagu näiteks ellu ärkavad maalid ja asukohta muutvad majad. Kas inimeste seas liiguvad tõesti ringi vereimejad või on kogu lugu ühelt tegelaselt, riiginõunik Teljajevilt hinnangut laenates siiski „mingi allegooria, midagi metafoorset, hmm!“?\n\nJelena Skulskaja saatesõna valgustab A.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/50071.Bohumil_Hrabal">About Bohumil Hrabal</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/50071.Bohumil_Hrabal"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1255518886p3/50071.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/50071.Bohumil_Hrabal">Bohumil Hrabal
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
926
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer4390516142183611986">Born in Brno-Židenice, Moravia, he lived briefly in Polná, but was raised in the Nymburk brewery as the manager's stepson. <br/><br/>Hrabal received a Law degree from Prague's Charles University, and lived in the city from the late 1940s on.<br/><br/>He worked as a manual laborer alongside Vladimír Boudník in the Kladno ironworks in the 1950s, an experience which inspired the "hyper-realist" texts he was writing at t</span>
  <span id="freeText4390516142183611986" style="display:none">Born in Brno-Židenice, Moravia, he lived briefly in Polná, but was raised in the Nymburk brewery as the manager's stepson. <br /><br />Hrabal received a Law degree from Prague's Charles University, and lived in the city from the late 1940s on.<br /><br />He worked as a manual laborer alongside Vladimír Boudník in the Kladno ironworks in the 1950s, an experience which inspired the "hyper-realist" texts he was writing at the time. <br /><br />His best known novels were Closely Watched Trains (1965) and I Served the King of England. In 1965 he bought a cottage in Kersko, which he used to visit till the end of his life, and where he kept cats ("kočenky"). <br /><br />He was a great storyteller; his popular pub was At the Golden Tiger (U zlatého tygra) on Husova Street in Prague, where he met the Czech President Václav Havel, the American President Bill Clinton and the then-US ambassador to the UN Madeleine Albright on January 11th, 1994.<br /><br />Several of his works were not published in Czechoslovakia due to the objections of the authorities, including The Little Town Where Time Stood Still (Městečko, kde se zastavil čas) and I Served the King of England (Obsluhoval jsem anglického krále).<br /><br />He died when he fell from a fifth floor hospital where he was apparently trying to feed pigeons. It was noted that Hrabal lived on the fifth floor of his apartment building and that suicides by leaping from a fifth-floor window were mentioned in several of his books.<br /><br />He was buried in a family grave in the cemetery in Hradištko. In the same grave his mother "Maryška", step father "Francin", uncle "Pepin", wife "Pipsi" and brother "Slávek" were buried.<br /><br />He wrote with an expressive, highly visual style, often using long sentences; in fact his work Dancing Lessons for the Advanced in Age (1964) (Taneční hodiny pro starší a pokročilé) is made up of just one sentence. Many of Hrabal's characters are portrayed as "wise fools" - simpletons with occasional or inadvertent profound thoughts - who are also given to coarse humour, lewdness, and a determination to survive and enjoy oneself despite harsh circumstances. Political quandaries and their concomitant moral ambiguities are also a recurrent theme.<br /><br />Along with Jaroslav Hašek, Karel Čapek, and Milan Kundera - who were also imaginative and amusing satirists - he is considered one of the greatest Czech writers of the 20th century. His works have been translated into 27 languages.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4390516142183611986" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/284719-p-li-hlu-n-samota">Quotes from Too Loud a Solitude</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;Because when I read, I don't really read; I pop a beautiful sentence into my mouth and suck it like a fruit drop, or I sip it like a liqueur until the thought dissolves in me like alcohol, infusing brain and heart and coursing on through the veins to the root of each blood vessel.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/370980-because-when-i-read-i-don-t-really-read-i-pop">293 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;I can be by myself because I'm never lonely; I'm simply alone, living in my heavily populated solitude, a harum-scarum of infinity and eternity, and Infinity and Eternity seem to take a liking to the likes of me.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
